        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.3c03340"><meta property="og:title" content="Hexagonal Sb Nanocrystals as High-Capacity and Long-Cycle Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c03340/asset/images/acsami.3c03340.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Antimony (Sb) is regarded as a promising anode material for sodium ion batteries (SIBs) on account of its high theoretical specific capacity (∼660 mAh g–1) and low cost. However, the large volume expansion (∼390%) during charging has inhibited its practical application. Herein, hexagonal Sb nanocrystals encapsulated by P/N-co-doped carbon nanofibers (Sb@P-N/C) were prepared using a low-cost but mass-produced electrospinning method. The as-prepared Sb@P-N/C, used as anode material for SIBs, exhibits unexpected cycling stability and rate capability, with 500.1 mAh g–1 at 50 mA g–1 after 200 cycles and 295.6 mAh g–1 at 500 mA g–1 after 400 cycles. Especially, the full battery fabricated by Na (Ni1/3Fe1/3Mn1/3) O2 || Sb@P-N/C possesses a reversible specific capacity of 66.8 mAh g–1 at 50 mA g–1 over 60 cycles. This simple and low-cost fabrication technology combined with unique crystal morphology offers new strategies for the advancement of sodium ion batteries (SIBs) in energy storage and electrical transportation."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c03340/asset/images/acsami.3c03340.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="931882c73c6854b8-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="931882c73c6854b8-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '5c59f018-a567-4ff1-a96c-91fdcf06e519'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

src="/specs/products/achs/releasedAssets/public/mathjax-0a9852ad89643b94fe5099b753f6a294/MathJax.js?config=TeX-AMS-MML_HTMLorMML"></script><script type="text/x-mathjax-config;executed=true">MathJax.Hub.Config({
            menuSettings: {
                zoom: "Click",
                zscale: "150%"
            },
            SVG: {
                fonts: ["STIX-Web"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
            },
            "HTML-CSS": {
                fonts: ["STIX"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
                scale: 90,
            },
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("mml Jax Ready", function () {
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            MML.mi.prototype.setTeXclass = function (prev) {
                this.getPrevClass(prev);
                return this;
            };
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                setTeXclass: function (prev) {
                    this.getPrevClass(prev);
                    for (var i = 0, m = this.data.length; i < m; i++) {
                        if (this.data[i]) this.data[i].setTeXclass();
                    }
                    return this;
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("SVG Jax Ready", function () {
            var FONT = MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"] || MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"];
            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("MathML Jax Ready", function () {
            var PARSE = MathJax.InputJax.MathML.Parse;
            var ADD = PARSE.prototype.AddChildren;
            PARSE.Augment({
                AddChildren: function (mml, node) {
                    ADD.call(this, mml, node);
                    if (mml.type === 'mrow') {
                        var first = mml.data[0], last = mml.data[mml.data.length-1];
                        if (mml.open && !mml.data[0].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.open;
                        if (mml.close && !mml.data[mml.data.length - 1].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.close;
                    }
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS Jax Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax['HTML-CSS'];
            var DELIMITERS = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.DELIMITERS;
            var FONT = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"] || HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"]

            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;

            MML.mrow.Augment({
              _HTMLlineBreaks: MML.mrow.prototype.HTMLlineBreaks,
              HTMLlineBreaks: function (span) {

                if (this.parent.linebreakContainer && this.Get("display") === "inline") return false;
                return this._HTMLlineBreaks(span);
              }
            });


            DELIMITERS[0x0305] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x00AF};
            DELIMITERS[0x033F] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x003D};
            DELIMITERS[0x20E1] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x2194};

            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS multiline Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax["HTML-CSS"];
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            var PENALTY = MML.mbase.prototype.HTMLlinebreakPenalty;

            MML.msqrt.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mroot.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mspace.Augment({
                defaultDef: {
                    indentalign: MML.INDENTALIGN.AUTO,
                    indentshift: "0",
                    indenttarget: "",
                    indentalignfirst: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftfirst: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT,
                    indentalignlast: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftlast: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT
                },
                HTMLbetterBreak: function (info,state) {
                    if (info.values && info.values.id === this.spanID) {return false}
                    var values = this.getValues(
                        "linebreak",
                        "indentalign","indentshift",
                        "indentalignfirst","indentshiftfirst",
                        "indentalignlast","indentshiftlast"
                    );
                    var linebreakValue = values.linebreak;
                    if (!linebreakValue || this.hasDimAttr()) {
                        linebreakValue = MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO;
                    }
                    var W = info.scanW, span = this.HTMLspanElement(), w = span.bbox.w;
                    if (span.style.paddingLeft) {w += HTMLCSS.unEm(span.style.paddingLeft)}
                    if (W - info.shift === 0) {return false} // don't break at zero width
                    var offset = HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth - W;

                    if (state.n === 0 && (values.indentshiftfirst !== state.VALUES.indentshiftfirst ||
                        values.indentalignfirst !== state.VALUES.indentalignfirst)) {
                        var align = this.HTMLgetAlign(state,values),
                            shift = this.HTMLgetShift(state,values,align);
                        offset += (info.shift - shift);
                    }

                    var penalty = Math.floor(offset / HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth * 1000);
                    if (penalty < 0) {penalty = PENALTY.toobig - 3*penalty}
                    penalty += info.nest * PENALTY.nestfactor;

                    var linebreak = PENALTY[linebreakValue]||0;
                    if (linebreakValue === MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO && w >= PENALTY.spacelimit &&
                        !this.mathbackground && !this.background)
                    {linebreak = [(w+PENALTY.spaceoffset)*PENALTY.spacefactor]}
                    if (!MathJax.Object.isArray(linebreak)) {
                        if (linebreak || offset >= 0) {penalty = linebreak * info.nest}
                    } else {penalty = Math.max(1,penalty + linebreak[0] * info.nest)}

                    if (penalty >= info.penalty) {return false}
                    info.penalty = penalty; info.values = values; info.W = W; info.w = w;
                    values.lineleading = state.VALUES.lineleading;
                    values.linebreakstyle = "before"; values.id = this.spanID;
                    return true;
                }
            });


            
            PENALTY.goodbreak = [-400];

        });</script><style type="text/css">.MathJax_Hover_Frame {border-radius: .25em; -webkit-border-radius: .25em; -moz-border-radius: .25em; -khtml-border-radius: .25em; box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -webkit-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -moz-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -khtml-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; border: 1px solid #A6D ! important; display: inline-block; position: absolute}

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="931882c73c6854b8-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

<script nonce="931882c73c6854b8-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Hexagonal Sb Nanocrystals as High-Capacity and Long-Cycle Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c03340", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Hexagonal Sb Nanocrystals as High-Capacity and Long-Cycle Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c03340", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Hexagonal Sb Nanocrystals as High-Capacity and Long-Cycle Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c03340", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Hexagonal Sb Nanocrystals as High-Capacity and Long-Cycle Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c03340", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Hexagonal Sb Nanocrystals as High-Capacity and Long-Cycle Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c03340", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Hexagonal Sb Nanocrystals as High-Capacity and Long-Cycle Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c03340", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Hexagonal Sb Nanocrystals as High-Capacity and Long-Cycle Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c03340", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Hexagonal Sb Nanocrystals as High-Capacity and Long-Cycle Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c03340", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Hexagonal Sb Nanocrystals as High-Capacity and Long-Cycle Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c03340", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

University of Science and Technology, 666 Changhui Road, Zhenjiang 212100, China</div></div><div class="loa-info-scifinder"><span>More by <a href="/action/doSearch?field1=Contrib&amp;text1=Jiahao++Xu" data-id="article_author_search">Jiahao Xu</a></span></div></div></div></span></li><li><span><span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor" tabindex="0" data-id="article_author_info" property="author">Pengfei He</span><div class="loa-info hlFld-Affiliation"><div class="loa-info--wrapper"><div class="loa-info-name">Pengfei He</div><div class="loa-info-affiliations"><div class="loa-info-affiliations-info">School of Aerospace Engineering and Applied Mechanics, Tongji University, 1239 Siping Road, Shanghai, 200082, China</div></div><div class="loa-info-scifinder"><span>More by <a href="/action/doSearch?field1=Contrib&amp;text1=Pengfei++He" data-id="article_author_search">Pengfei He</a></span></div><div class="loa-info-orcid"><img Ding</span><strong>*</strong><div class="loa-info hlFld-Affiliation"><div class="loa-info--wrapper"><div class="loa-info-name">Xuli Ding</div><div class="loa-info-affiliations"><div class="conrtib-corresp"><strong>*</strong>Email: <a href="mailto:xuliding@just.edu.cn">xuliding@just.edu.cn</a></span><span class="conrtib-corresp"></span><div class="loa-info-scifinder"><span>More by <a href="/action/doSearch?field1=Contrib&amp;text1=Xuli++Ding" data-id="article_author_search">Xuli Ding</a></span></div><div class="loa-info-orcid"><img height="20" width="27" However, the large volume expansion (∼390%) during charging has inhibited its practical application. Herein, hexagonal Sb nanocrystals encapsulated by P/N-co-doped carbon nanofibers (Sb@P-N/C) were prepared using a low-cost but mass-produced electrospinning method. The as-prepared Sb@P-N/C, used as anode material for SIBs, exhibits unexpected cycling stability and rate capability, with 500.1 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> after 200 cycles and 295.6 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup> after 400 cycles. Especially, the full battery fabricated by Na (Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>) O<sub>2</sub> || Sb@P-N/C possesses a reversible specific capacity of 66.8 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> over 60 cycles. This simple and low-cost fabrication technology combined with unique crystal morphology offers new strategies for the advancement of sodium ion batteries (SIBs) in energy storage and electrical transportation.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D26728%26pageCount%3D9%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DNing%2BZhang%252C%2BXiaojing%2BChen%252C%2BJiahao%2BXu%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D15%26issueNum%3D22%26contentID%3Dacsami.3c03340%26title%3DHexagonal%2BSb%2BNanocrystals%2Bas%2BHigh-Capacity%2Band%2BLong-Cycle%2BAnode%2BMaterials%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D9%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D26736%26publicationDate%3DJune%2B2023" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2023 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries"> Batteries </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291338&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrical properties"> Electrical properties </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290667&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Ions"> Ions </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291451&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Nanocrystals"> Nanocrystals </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Antimony&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_antimony">antimony</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Electrospinning&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_electrospinning">electrospinning</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anode">anode</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Full+Battery&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_full%20battery">full battery</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The high-quality development of the energy storage industry has become an essential part in the era of carbon neutrality. Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have dominated the e-market in the past decades due to their high energy density and widespread use in electric vehicles and electronic equipment. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 1 to 8">(1−8)</a> However, limited by the lithium resource’s insufficient reserves, exorbitant price, and regional distribution, LIBs are incapable of meeting the rising demand for the efficient storage of renewables in an economical way. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 9 to 12">(9−12)</a> Meanwhile, the element sodium, which is abundant, inexpensive, and chemically similar to lithium, has attracted the attention of researchers, and then, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are recognized as the most potential substitute to LIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 13 to 16">(13−16)</a> Nevertheless, the larger radius of Na<sup>+</sup> ion and its heavier atomic mass than that of Li<sup>+</sup> ion (<i>r</i><sub>Na+</sub> = <sub>0.102&nbsp;nm</sub>/<i>r</i><sub>Li+</sub> = <sub>0.076&nbsp;nm</sub>; <i>m</i><sub>Na&nbsp;=&nbsp;22.99&nbsp;g/mol</sub>/<i>m</i><sub>Li</sub> = <sub>6.94&nbsp;g/mol</sub>) make it difficult to be inserted into commercial graphite anodes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 17 to 23">(17−23)</a> Therefore, it is highly anticipated to explore high-capacity anode and cathode materials to boost the next generation of high=specific energy SIBs. Accordingly, metal antimony (Sb) is deemed a potential anode material for the high-energy density SIBs owing to its high theoretical specific capacity (∼660 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) and medium operating voltage (0.5–0.8 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 24 to 28">(24−28)</a> However, the excessive volume expansion in the process of charging (Sb → Na<sub>3</sub>Sb: 390%) and discharging usually leads to the crushing and collapse of the fabricated electrodes as well as the weakening of the electrical contact. We repeated the regeneration of the solid electrolyte interface (SEI) layer, which inevitably resulted in its severe capacity decrease and unsatisfactory cyclability. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29 ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 29 to 33">(29−33)</a> In consequence, various strategies have been carried out to combine Sb with other metals and carbon compounds to strengthen its cycle stability, such as Sb/Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> BiSb@Bi<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>/SbO<sub><i>x</i></sub>@C, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/CNTs, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> CoSb, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> NiSb, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> ZnSb, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> and SnSb, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> which push greatly the progress in the cycle life and charging/discharging capacity of Sb-based SIB anodes. Despite this, to date, few reports about the hexagonal Sb nanocrystal are especially encapsulated by P/N co-doped carbon nanofibers by electrospinning.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In this work, we fabricated hexagonal Sb nanocrystals embedded in P/N co-doped C fibers (Sb@P-N/C) via a simple electrospinning method combined with a heating processing strategy. It is generally considered that the hexagonal crystal structures can produce a strong mechanically stable support and the total surface tension of the edges is minimal. It is critical for the electrochemical performance and lifetime of batteries to maintain the structural integrity of Sb-based composites during charging and discharging. Noticeably, the hexagonal Sb nanocrystals are 200 nm–1 μm in size and embedded in the fabricated Sb@P-N/C nanofibers. Importantly, the P/N co-doping can play a synergistic effect for Na<sup>+</sup> ion storage, as P-doping exhibiting stronger adsorption capacity and high theoretical specific capacity (corresponding to Na<sub>3</sub>P; ∼2596 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>), while N-doping increases more active dots for Na<sup>+</sup> ion storage and electronic conductivity of charge transfer. In addition, the P-N/C nanofibers can also buffer Sb’s volume expansion during sodiation, achieving stable cycling properties for the fabricated Sb@P-N/C composite anodes. Moreover, the 1D fibers exhibited a high aspect ratio and satisfactory transport interconnectivity, which are significant for Na<sup>+</sup> ion storage and transfer. Importantly, the contribution of the N/P co-doped carbon matrix is mainly summarized as follows: (1) provide more charge transport channels; (2) shorten the Na<sup>+</sup> ions’ transfer distance; (3) provide more active position for Na<sup>+</sup> ion storage; (4) improve the mechanical strength and toughness of the fabricated composite electrode. Consequently, the fabricated Sb@P-N/C anode exhibits a high reversible specific capacity and prolonged cycle life. Obviously, it demonstrates a reversible specific capacity of 500.1 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 200 cycles at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> and 295.6 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> following 400 cycles at 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. Furthermore, the reversible specific capacity of the Na(Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>)O<sub>2</sub>∥Sb@P-N/C full battery in a 0.05 C loop for 60 cycles is 66.8 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, which means a significant value for its use in SIBs.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Fabrication of the Sb@P-N/C Nanocomposite</h3><div class="NLM_p last">One micromole of SbCl<sub>3</sub> (Aladdin, <i>M</i><sub>w</sub> = 228.12, 99%), 1 mmol of triphenylphosphine (Sinopharm Chemical Reagent Co, Ltd., <i>M</i><sub>w</sub> = 262.29, 99.5%), and 1 mmol of urea (Sinopharm Chemical Reagent Co, Ltd., <i>M</i><sub>w</sub> = 60.06, 99.0%) were mixed with 10 mL of <i>N</i>,<i>N</i>-dimethyl formamide (DMF) in a beaker with continuous stirring for 10 h until the mixture became transparent. After that, 0.8 g of polyvinylpyrrolidone (PVP, Aladdin, 10% (w/v)) and 0.75 g of polymethyl methacrylate (PMMA, Aladdin, (w/v)) were placed into the beaker and continuously stirred for 12 h to generate a uniformly mixed solution. The obtained solution was transferred into a 10 mL injector for the subsequent electrostatic spinning operation. Generally, the electrospinning parameter was set as follows: (1) The positive and negative voltages of electrospinning are +18 and – 3 kV; (2) there was an approximately 15 cm distance between the needle and the roller; (3) the stream speed of the injector was 0.08 mm/min. After spinning, the obtained precursors were wrapped in aluminum foil and placed in an oven at 50 °C overnight and then subjected to carbonization in a tube furnace. Initially, the temperature was brought up to 100 °C with a rising speed of 1 °C min<sup>–1</sup> and then maintained at 100 °C for 1 h. Heating was continued at the same rate until 300 °C. Afterward, the ramp rate of 5 °C min<sup>–1</sup> up to 550 °C at was kept for 2 h for carbonization. All steps were done under the protection of Ar/H<sub>2</sub> mixture gas. The obtained sample was designated as Sb@P-N/C. For comparison, Sb@C (no triphenylphosphine and urea) and Sb@N/C (with urea) were also prepared under the same conditions.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2.  Morphology and Structure Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The microstructure of the fabricated Sb@P-N/C was evaluated by transmission electron microscopy (TEM) and high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) via a JEOL JEM-2100 instrument with a CCD camera. Moreover, scanning electron microscopy (SEM) was used to investigate the overall structure of the composite. Raman spectra (Renishaw) were measured with a laser intensity of 5 mW and wavelength of 532 nm. The phase structure evolution of Sb@P-N/C was studied by an X’Pert PRO diffractometer (XRD), Shimadzu, in the range of 5–85° at a scanning rate of 5° min<sup>–1</sup> using λ = 0.15406 nm. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, Escalb 250xi) was utilized to further analyze the samples’ elemental composition and chemical valence states. The thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) test was undertaken in air at a heating rate of 10 °C min<sup>–1</sup> within the temperature range of 25 to 800 °C using a zero-friction vertical dilatometer (L75VS Lin-seis, Germany).</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.3.  Battery Assembly and Electrochemical Performance Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Coin cells of CR2032 are adopted in the assembly of both the half-cell and the full-cell. Whatman GF/D membrane was used as the separator, and sodium foil was used as the counter electrode. The electrolyte consists of 1.0 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> dissolved in 1:1 EC/DMC with 5% FEC added. The working electrode is composed of 70 wt % active materials (Sb@P-N/C), 15 wt % carbon black (Super P), and 15 wt % sodium alginate mixed in an agate mortar and ground together for 25 min. After that, the slurry was painted onto the copper foil and baked in a 70 °C oven for 8–10 h. The mass loading of the electrode materials is 0.8–1.5 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. The cells are assembled in an Ar-filled glove box with an oxygen and water concentration of less than 0.1 ppm. The fabricated cells are laid aside for 8–10 h before the electrochemical performance measurement. Electrochemical impedance spectrum (EIS) with a frequency spanning from 1 MHz to 0.1 Hz, and cyclic voltammetry (CV) measurement at a sweeping rate ranging from 0.1 to 0.5 mV s<sup>–1</sup> were both conducted using a DH7001 instrument. The charging/discharging cycling curves were also measured on Landt (Wuhan) and Neware (Shenzhen) battery cabinets. Particularly, the half-cell measurement potential is set in 0.01–1.5 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na, and the full-cell potential is in the range of 2.0–3.5 V.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a> illustrates the TEM, HRTEM, and SAED images of the Sb@P-N/C nanocomposite to investigate its internal microstructure. As shown, the hexagonal Sb nanocrystals (yellow dashed lines) and nanoribbons (green dashed lines) are clearly visible in the TEM images. Obviously, the hexagonal Sb nanocrystals are in the size range of 200 nm–1 μm; most of them are located on the surface of the carbon fibers, and some are embedded inside the inner and the Sb nanoribbons that are occasionally detected in the size range of 600 nm–1.5 μm, interspersed in the carbon fibers side-by-side with the hexagonal Sb nanocrystals in a well-defined distribution. It deserves to be mentioned that the distribution of carbon fibers around the Sb nanocrystals not only provides space to accommodate the volume expansion of Sb during sodiation but also hinders the aggregation of Sb nanocrystals, which warrants the integrity of the electrode structure. In the HRTEM images shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>e–g, the ordered parallel stripes with varied spacing distances are clearly distinguishable, which belong to different crystalline planes of Sb. As indicated, the stripe distances of 0.31, 0.22, and 0.21 nm represent the lattice faces of Sb (012), Sb (104), and Sb (110), respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41 ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41 ref42" aria-label="reference 41 and 42">(41,42)</a> The result is also consistent with the XRD patterns. More importantly, all the stripes are highly parallel, indicating the unexceptionable degree of crystallinity of the synthesized Sb nanocrystals with regular hexagonal morphologies. Simultaneously, as depicted in the SAED image in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>h, the diffraction spots form a hexagonal shape, which further confirms the monocrystal nature of the prepared sample. In addition, the SEM images with diverse magnifications for Sb@P-N/C are provided in Supporting Information <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c03340/suppl_file/am3c03340_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>. It is observed that the carbon fibers are distributed overall with a spider web-like structure, which increases the contact area between the electrolyte and the active matter and provides good electrical conductivity. More details of the analysis are presented in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c03340/suppl_file/am3c03340_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>. In particular, Supporting Information <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c03340/suppl_file/am3c03340_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a> provides SEM images after 100 cycles. Meanwhile, the HRTEM images and TEM images after 200 cycles are further provided in supporting <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c03340/suppl_file/am3c03340_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>. In addition to this, SEM images and electrochemical performance of Sb particles without nanofibers are also obtained in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c03340/suppl_file/am3c03340_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4 and S5</a>.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c03340/asset/images/medium/am3c03340_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c03340/asset/images/large/am3c03340_0002.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a–d) TEM images of the Sb@P-N/C composite; (e–g) HRTEM images of Sb@P-N/C; (h) selected-area electron diffraction (SAED) image of the composite material.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c03340/asset/images/large/am3c03340_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c03340&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In order to analyze the crystalline structure of the fabricated samples, X-ray diffraction (XRD) is performed, and the results are compared in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a. As revealed, the three compared samples are essentially identical and all the characteristic diffraction peaks corresponded exactly to the Sb phase (PDF no 85-1323). The diffraction peaks at 28.7, 40.2, and 41.9° correspond to the (012), (104), and (110) crystal planes of Sb, and no obvious impurity diffraction peak is observed, indicating that the obtained Sb phase has high purity and good crystallinity. Moreover, the XRD spectra after one cycle (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c03340/suppl_file/am3c03340_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>) and 100 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c03340/suppl_file/am3c03340_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>) are also provided in the Supporting Information. It can be observed from the plots that once the electrodes are cycled, the NaSb and Na<sub>3</sub>Sb phases appear in the disassembled batteries. Furthermore, the Raman spectra is measured and shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c03340/suppl_file/am3c03340_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8a</a>, and as shown, two distinct vibration peaks centered at 113 and 150 cm<sup>–1</sup> are associated with the E<sub>g</sub> and A<sub>1g</sub> vibration modes of Sb, which further proves the generation of the hexagonal Sb phase. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref44 ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43 ref44 ref45" aria-label="reference 43 to 45">(43−45)</a> In addition, two characteristic peaks located at 1356 and 1598 cm<sup>–1</sup> that are assigned to the D (disordered) and G (graphitic structure) of C, respectively, are present in the measurement spectra. What’s more important is that X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was also used to analyze the chemical elements and valence state variations of the fabricated materials. Clearly, the C 1s, O 1s, Sb 3d, N 1s, and P 2p signals are identified from the XPS full spectra in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b. Correspondingly, the presence of the O 1s signal is attributed to the surface oxidation of Sb and P, and the C 1s band with peaks centered at 284.8 and 286.6 eV, corresponds to the C═C and C–O bonds (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c), separately. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> Similarly, the high-resolution P 2p band as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d, the peaks at 131.8 and 134.3 eV relating to the P–P and P–O–C bonds, respectively, are also exhibited. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47 ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref47 ref48" aria-label="reference 47 and 48">(47,48)</a> Likewise, in the Sb 3d band, the Sb<sup>0</sup> 3d<sub>5/2</sub> and Sb<sup>0</sup> 3d<sub>3/2</sub> peaks that connected with the binding energies of 528.7 and 537.9 eV, respectively, are clearly distinct in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>e, while two peaks at 530.7 and 540.2 eV are relevant to the Sb<sup>3+</sup>3d<sub>5/2</sub> and Sb<sup>3+</sup>3d<sub>3/2</sub> peaks, separately. In addition, the Sb<sup>3+</sup>3d<sub>5/2</sub> peak coincides with the O 1s peak centered at 530.7 eV, which is also consistent with the previous reports that the Sb nanoparticle surface is usually oxidizable. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49 ref50" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49 ref50" aria-label="reference 49 and 50">(49,50)</a> More information about the element ratio can be found in Supporting Information <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c03340/suppl_file/am3c03340_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Tables S1 and S2</a>. Meanwhile, the high-resolution N 1s spectrum displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>f is decomposed into three distinct peaks, pyridinic-N at 398.7 eV, pyrrolic-N at 400.1 eV, and graphitic-N at 401.8 eV, respectively. The doping of N elements in the carbon fibers not only affords additional electrochemical active sites for Na<sup>+</sup> ions storage but also enhances the conductivity of carbon fibers to facilitate Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31 ref51" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31 ref51" aria-label="reference 31 and 51">(31,51)</a> In addition, the mass ratios of graphitic-N, pyrrolic-N, and pyridinic-N in the Sb@P-N/C composites are calculated to be 6.2, 52.1, and 41.7%, respectively. More information about the ratio of the N element is also provided in Supporting Information <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c03340/suppl_file/am3c03340_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S3</a>. In addition, the carbon content in the Sb@P-N/C sample is also determined using TGA (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c03340/suppl_file/am3c03340_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8b</a>), in which the carbon content is 72.1%, corresponding to the rapid weight loss occurring at 450–600 °C in the TGA curves that were attributed to the decomposition of carbon in the Sb@P-N/C composite.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c03340/asset/images/medium/am3c03340_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c03340/asset/images/large/am3c03340_0003.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) XRD spectrum of the Sb@C, Sb@N/C, and Sb@P-N/C nanomaterials, (b) XPS full spectrum of the Sb@C, Sb@N/C, and Sb@P-N/C nanomaterials, and high-resolution XPS spectra with Gaussian fitting of (c) C 1s, (d) P 2p, (e) Sb 3d, and (f) N 1s.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c03340/asset/images/large/am3c03340_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c03340&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To further explore the Na<sup>+</sup> ions’ reaction kinetics, the cyclic voltammetry property of the composite of Sb@P-N/C was investigated. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a presents the CV curves of the Sb@P-N/C electrode at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> in the voltage window of 0.01–2.0 V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>). During the initial discharge processing, a broad cathodic band is noticed at ∼0.18 V, which is attributable to the formation of solid electrolyte interface (SEI) layer. In the succeeding cycles, the reduction peaks at 0.68, 0.48, and 0.32 V are linked to the multi-step alloy reaction of Sb into Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sb and Na<sub>3</sub>Sb. In contrast, three anodic peaks at 0.70, 0.84, and 0.92 V are attributed to the dealloying reaction of the Na<sub>3</sub>Sb phase and the forming hexagonal Sb phase. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52 ref53 ref54 ref55" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref52 ref53 ref54 ref55" aria-label="reference 52 to 55">(52−55)</a> Comparably, the anodic peaks at 0.68 and 0.48 V correspond to the alloying reaction of Na<sub>3</sub>P, while the peaks at 0.70 and 0.92 V during the discharging process are attributed to the transformation of Na<sub>3</sub>P to the Na<sub>2</sub>P and NaP phases gradually, which is also consistent with previously reported results. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56 ref57 ref58" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref56 ref57 ref58" aria-label="reference 56 to 58">(56−58)</a> In addition, the good overlap property of different CV curves further certifies the high reversibility and cycle stability character of the fabricated Sb@P-N/C electrode. More details regarding the CV curves of Sb@C and Sb@N/C are displayed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c03340/suppl_file/am3c03340_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>. Meanwhile, the d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> curves of three samples, which also represent the change in peak position during charging and discharging, are also rendered in Supporting Information <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c03340/suppl_file/am3c03340_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c03340/asset/images/medium/am3c03340_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c03340/asset/images/large/am3c03340_0004.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Electrochemical performance of the sodium half-cell: (a) cyclic voltammetric curve (CV) at a scanning rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> for the first three cycles; (b) galvanostatic charging and discharging curves of Sb@P-N/C at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>; (c) comparison of the cycling performance of different samples at the current density of 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>; (d) rate capability compare of varied samples at high current densities from 30, 50, 100, 200, and 500 mAg<sup>–1</sup>, (e) cycling performance comparisons of Sb@P-N/C at varied current densities of 0.5, 1.0, and 5.0 A g<sup>–1</sup><sub>.</sub></p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c03340/asset/images/large/am3c03340_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c03340&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Totally, the alloying/dealloying reaction mechanism of Sb and P is described by the conversion as below: <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55 ref59" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref55 ref59" aria-label="reference 55 and 59">(55,59)</a><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display" id="MJXp-Span-1"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-2"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-3"><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-4"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-5">Sb</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-6" style="margin-left: 0.111em; margin-right: 0.267em;">+</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-16">x</span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-17"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-18" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-19">e</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-20" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-21">−</span><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-22">1</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mspace" id="MJXp-Span-23" style="width: 0.25em; height: 0em;"></span><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-24"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-25" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-26">Na</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-27" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-28">x</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-29">Sb</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-30" style="margin-left: 0.111em; margin-right: 0.111em;">/</span><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-31">P</span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-1"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>Sb</mi><mo>/</mo><mi mathvariant="normal">P</mi><mo>+</mo><mi>x</mi><msup><mrow><mi>Na</mi></mrow><mrow><mo>+</mo></mrow></msup><mo>+</mo><mi>x</mi><msup><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">e</mi></mrow><mrow><mo>−</mo><mn>1</mn></mrow></msup><mspace width="0.25em"></mspace><msub><mrow><mi>Na</mi></mrow><mrow><mi>x</mi></mrow></msub><mi>Sb</mi><mo>/</mo><mi mathvariant="normal">P</mi></mstyle></mrow></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display" id="MJXp-Span-32"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-33"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-34"><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-35"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-36"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-37" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-38">Na</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-39" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-40">x</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-41">Sb</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-42" style="margin-left: 0.111em; margin-right: 0.267em;">+</span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-58"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-59" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em;">(</span><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-60">3</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-61" style="margin-left: 0.267em;
